MK/ADVENTURE

Every two years come January, Washington is an extraordinarily busy city. Frank Church had long since grown used to this fact after twenty years in the United States Senate, he was more than used to seeing a freshman class of Congressmen sworn in every two years in the snow blanketed capital, staff rushing across the streets and towards the Capitol building so they can claim a half-decent office before some other bum fresh-eyed wet-behind-the-ear first-termer takes it. He could only look back fondly on his own early days, when it all seemed much simpler.

Every four years come January is a different story, when even the most burrowed politicians can’t escape the whirlwind of drunken rumors, secret meetings, and punditry over whatever ghoulish figure is preparing to take the White House this time. It was, of course, better when it was your own party. Church noted the mood in camp Democrat in the Senate to be a great deal brighter than his friends across the aisle, where he only found a solemn and dull Barry Goldwater sitting in the dark while Howard Baker plotted on how he’d flip a Humphrey presidency into his own fortune come the ever present “next time.”

It was all anyone seemed to be worried about in this line of work. He looked on as Walter Mondale and Adlai Stevenson, two members of the Humphrey Inauguration Committee, walked across the salted street, smiling joyfully. Of course Mondale was smiling, Church mused, he was next in line for the top job “next time.”

It was an open secret in D.C. that a Humphrey administration could not possibly last more than four years, and that it may not get to January 1981 in the first place. Therefor, anyone taking jobs or affiliating themselves with the sickly incoming President Humphrey must be working to further their own career, to prepare for that abhorrent “next time.”

He grimaced at the thought, walking faster to catch up to his two Democratic colleagues. Frank Church, obviously, wasn’t above blatant careerism. He had spent his earliest years in the chamber trying not to step on the toes of Lyndon Johnson, he had gone to war with the intelligence establishment, with the whispers that it’d be him preparing to take office later this month. However, that was it. His next time in the past now, the hopes and dreams of a Church presidency that he had so longed for since he was young and looked up at beautiful pictures of his idols such as Franklin Roosevelt and William Borah, those who really made the difference.

The Senator from Idaho would be lying if he said it wasn’t a nasty feeling—what with that mess at the convention—it had been taken right from under his nose. Not only the presidency, but so much more. Walter Mondale had (in his kindest words) gave Church the word bluntly. There could be intelligence reform in the Humphrey term, but it’d be nowhere as broad or as deep as Church had been planning for his own administration. “The President-Elect wants to tackle the bigger issues.” The administration’s would-be chief ally in the Senate said, shaking his head. “Don’t be worried, Frank, we’ve been working with Jerry Ford on a handful of smaller reforms.” Humphrey assured the unusually quiet Church only a few days ago, before inviting him to join the Inauguration Committee.

It was enough to make him sick.

With each swing of the door, the whole barroom shivered. A gale force wind blasted through this old Minneapolis speakeasy, plunging the inside temperature down to what it was outside: somewhere between zero and the Arctic tundras. If you sat close enough to the entrance, every time a new patron came into the relative warmth, you could even see your own breath.

That is precisely why Walter Mondale sat as far from the door as he possibly could. Nursing a bottle of rum, the Minnesota Senator was not yet 50 and already looked as if he had aged a thousand years. That’s what losing will do to anyone, he thought grimly. The Senator took another swig. Gathered around this funeral affair were a handful of political operatives and old party hands, who all thought their man would finally make it over the top this time. That maybe this year, old Hubert had it in him. They were all sorely mistaken.

Not a soul present that day would forget when Lyndon Johnson had raced from the floor of the Senate, as the George substitute to the Bricker amendment hung in the balance, only to find that Harvey Kilgore of West Virginia had fallen asleep in his office after another bender. Literally dragging the hungover final vote to the floor, Johnson successfully killed that amendment. It was an old tale Humphrey loved to entertain his friends with.

Mondale knew he’d be a top contender, if the stain of this loss had washed away by then. If not, maybe Bayh or Kennedy, or someone totally new, maybe one of those Watergate Babies.

It was a nice fantasy. Mondale knew where it would inevitably land. He had run away from his fate this cycle, said some cheap line about not wanting to sleep in Holiday Inns every night. But he winced the moment the words left his mouth. He knew what the press would all say. Would Lincoln have shirked his duties like that? Did Roosevelt turn away from the siren call because he was tired of rubber chicken dinners? Had the thought even occurred to Kennedy that the mild inconveniences of a campaign dissuade him from serving his nation? What hurt most was that they were all right. Mondale knew he had run like a coward when his country needed him. Had he been the nominee instead of Hubert…

Mondale let the thought linger there. He didn’t dare come to the same conclusion everyone else at the table had. But next time it would be different. He was sure of it.

PB/PRIME

Inside the glittering ballroom of the Sheraton Hotel, all Washington’s chattering class could gossip about was who was seated at the table closest to the stage. They traded furtive glances in-between second go-arounds at the caviar. Could it be? Was this man finally leaving his office to go and…socialize? It was as if something had defied human nature. Defied even God’s will. The very thought of him sitting there, watching these stale skits about moonshine, or that two-bit comedian imitating drunken husbands returning home to their wives, why it was simply unthinkable. But there he was.

Robert Carlyle Byrd never was one to attend these galas. He never indulged in the stripteases or booze-filled clubby nights on the town his colleagues did. In his quarter century on the Hill, he has attended three baseball games—two of them were on the same day, a double header—one football game—he had to crown the Queen at halftime—and one film, which he walked out on halfway through. When Ted Kennedy left his spot as Majority Whip for a weekend to go skiing, Byrd schemed to throw him off his post, lock down the votes to take the job for himself, and won. When Kennedy returned, he found himself stripped of his titles.

Now Byrd found himself in a similar spot. He scowled at the tawdry acts on stage. He permitted himself to eavesdrop on the hushed conversations surrounding him, if only to get a sense of chemistry. To identify his colleagues’ weak spots. To sense where they expected him to fall. Oh he heard them alright. Hart and Leahy and Bumpers. They were nothing but children, Byrd thought with a curl of the lip. Babies, really. That’s what the press kept calling them after all.

Hell, the President-elect was no different. Another pious man trying to shake up the system. Bullshit, Byrd thought. He had spent every day of his life building this system. Church talked about serving in war. Byrd welded the ships that took him to China with his own bare hands. They were all walking around the world Robert Byrd created. They didn’t do a goddamn thing.

To hear Church sit there and lecture him, after barely winning, about “the way this is going to be” made Byrd want to scream. But he didn’t have to. He wasn’t enough of a child to lash out that way. No. He sat. And he stewed. And he schemed. Oh lord, did he scheme, Byrd thought with a smile creeping across his face. It didn’t hurt that his enemies made it so damn easy. It was as simple as moving around pieces on a chessboard, but even if they were Lyndon Johnson and Sam Rayburn themselves, Byrd would outwork them anyway.

He watched the moronic emcee onstage say something about what a “great honor it is to welcome” Byrd to this event. The Majority Whip only sighed, stood up, turned to face the applauding crowd with a grimace, then walked up to shake the man’s hand. Byrd took his prepared speech out of his suit jacket. The perfunctory verbal nods of thanks at his hosts. He could hardly remember which of those organizations was hosting the dinner. Not that he cared.

Byrd sighed, and began to read the text he had prepared. “I’d like to talk to you about what I believe our most pressing national crisis is,” he intoned. “It’s crime.” A deafening silence fell over the crowd. Byrd could detect a few chortles from the tables near the back. The whispering made him grit his teeth. Not just at what he was saying, but how he was saying it. “Crahm,” he could pick up someone chuckling to the woman next to them, imitating the way he spoke.

Byrd closed his eyes for a moment, folded his prepared speech up, and tucked it back away. He wasn’t going to read what his secretary had typed the night before. He was going to speak directly to these ungrateful buffoons.

“The proliferation of crime all across our country can be largely attributed to one fact: there is so little punishment for it.” More silence, then a far away cough. “The best way—and perhaps the only way—to halt the crime wave that has been getting worse and worse is to take those criminals, those animals, and instead of locking them up, we need to be putting them six feet under.” Byrd’s voice rose. “And for the ones we slap on the wrist, for the ones we don’t even bother to book and charge, I say that it is better to build more prisons and hire more jailers than it is to allow American cities to deteriorate further into racial jungles.” A few scattered claps, Byrd noticed, from the usual old timers, like Eastland with his bottle of bourbon, but also some of the younger cohort. Byrd smirked. He was winning them over at last. Now he would twist the knife.

“So when I hear our President-elect,” Byrd annunciated slowly, drawing out every vowel in ‘president,’ “talk about crahm, while he’s out there swimming in the Gulf Coast, I think each and every one of us ought to be crystal clear about how serious a national emergency this is. This isn’t something you can sleep through.” The applause was louder this time. “You can’t talk to a couple’a magazine writers and talk tough, then go out there fishin’ back home in Idaho.” Some of the Southerners were cheering now. But this wasn’t just a Good Ole Boys rally, this was something different.

“Church says he wants to ‘change how things are done in this town,’ well let me tell you this, Senator,” Byrd hissed. “I have never in my life played a game of cards. I have never in my life had a golf club in my hand. I have never in life hit a tennis ball. I have never thrown a line over to catch a fish. I don't do any of those things. I have only had to work all my life.” The cheering was overpowering his speech now. He could feel it in his bones. At last, he had awakened these recalcitrant members of the Congress back to life. WI/ROGUE

“Must have been pretty tough for you folks last night, wasn’t it?”

Leo Ryan grimaced. He was never overly fond of the backslapping old guard of the House, especially not from a Republican like J. Edward Roush, who couldn’t even hold on to his own seat in a primary against some untested newcomer. Ryan only rolled his eyes and nodded, murmuring in response.

He would need another pot of coffee to get through the morning. Ryan hadn’t even been up that late the night before. All the networks had called it early, once it became clear which way the mood of the country was going. Ryan wasn’t dumb. He knew the nation was on a rightward lurch ever since the moment Richard Nixon rode back into the White House six years earlier. But Christ, Jerry Ford? Really? It was an affront to his intelligence.

Ryan pitied the man he ran against. The California Congressman was hardly a close friend of Frank Church, but the two cared deeply about oversight of America’s secret police—otherwise known as the “intelligence community,” as those smug spies always corrected him in Congressional testimony. Ryan couldn’t stand the sight of them, and neither could Church. Their work had overlapped in the last few years, when Frank was leading his own Senate Committee, and Ryan was working with Iowa’s Harold Hughes to pass legislation clamping down on those intelligence abuses. The very ones which led to Watergate, Vietnam, and now Angola.

It only made the result more maddening. Ryan thought McGovern had been a well-meaning and intelligent man, but he could read his own district well enough. Even the suburban white collar voters who cast their ballots for Ryan couldn’t stomach the antiwar dove at the top of the ticket. Ryan figured that if well-off voters who commute to San Francisco every day, and who despise right-wingers like Nixon and Reagan couldn’t pull the lever for a man like McGovern, then the South Dakotan never had a chance at the White House. But Church wasn’t like McGovern. Church came from one of the most conservative states in the nation. Church had right-wing stances on abortion and guns. Church was a brilliant orator who could bring any crowd to its feet. How could such a man fall so far?

It made Ryan sick to his stomach. He had always been passionate about the art of legislating. That wasn’t what bothered him. It was the endless campaigning that Ryan loathed. Enduring the constant criticism from liberal interest groups, getting death threats from fanatical California fascists, having to grovel and beg for endorsements at rubber chicken dinners. It was humiliating work.

Ryan looked back over his shoulder at Roush again. The Indiana Congressman was a lame duck now, set to leave Congress by the sixth of January. Ryan was never friendly with the man, but he began to wonder if Roush was getting off easy. This place could drain the life out of you, and with another one of Nixon’s lackeys given four more years, Ryan began to consider whether he should punch his ticket on the way out too. An early retirement didn’t sound so bad, all things considered. Ryan could return to his district and coast for however many years he had left, waking up every morning to the mist from the Bay rolling his suburban home in Hillsborough, gazing out at San Francisco from his perch.

It sounded like paradise to Ryan. But he also knew the voters of his district had elected him to another term. He had a duty to fill it out, no matter how much he hated it. “Information and Individual Rights,” Ryan finished, impatiently.

“Right, right,” Roush nodded. “Well, you better get acquainted with him. He’s still wet behind the ears. Gonna be in this town a long time, I think.”

Ryan sighed and nodded, turning away. Well, he thought, maybe a youngster could be useful. Ryan was in his fifties now, and he had no taste for the kind of barnburning spectacles his more liberal, youthful colleagues made of the Congress. “Oh…well that would be Danforth Quayle, of course.”

}); obs.observe(document.documentElement, { childList: true, subtree: true }); window.__ctRunningMatePileObserverV1 = obs; }; boot(); } enableRunningMatePhotoPile(); function getTooltips(str) { let matches = []; tooltipList.forEach((tooltip, index) => { let regex = new RegExp(`(?<=\\b|\\s|^|“)${tooltip.searchString}(?=[.,;!?]?\\b|\\s|”|$)`, 'g'); let match; while ((match = regex.exec(str)) !== null) { matches.push({ start: match.index + (match[0].startsWith('“') ? 1 : 0), end: match.index + match[0].length - (match[0].endsWith('”') ? 1 : 0) - (match[2] ? 1 : 0), tooltipIndex: index }); } }); matches.sort((a, b) => a.start - b.start || b.end - b.start - (a.end - a.start)); for (let i = 0; i < matches.length - 1; ) { if (matches[i + 1].start < matches[i].end) { matches.splice(i + 1, 1); } else { i++; } } return matches; } function applyTooltips(str) { const matches = getTooltips(str); let result = ''; let lastIndex = 0; matches.forEach(match => { const tooltip = tooltipList[match.tooltipIndex]; const safeExplanation = tooltip.explanationText .replace(//gi, "") .replace(/<\/div>/gi, ""); result += str.slice(lastIndex, match.start); result += `${tooltip.searchString}${safeExplanation}`; lastIndex = match.end; }); result += str.slice(lastIndex); return result; } function applyTooltipsToObject(obj) { for (let key in obj) { if (typeof obj[key] === 'string') { obj[key] = applyTooltips(obj[key]); } else if (typeof obj[key] === 'object') { applyTooltipsToObject(obj[key]); } } } applyTooltipsToObject(campaignTrail_temp.questions_json); applyTooltipsToObject(campaignTrail_temp.answers_json); applyTooltipsToObject(campaignTrail_temp.answer_feedback_json);
